If you&amp;#8217;re thankful for WordPress please let us know why in the comments. From all of us at the Tavern, have a safe and enjoyable Thanksgiving.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gratitude is a currency that we can mint for ourselves, and spend without fear of bankruptcy &amp;#8211; Fred De Witt Van Amburgh&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;div id=&quot;epoch-width-sniffer&quot;&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:7:&quot;pubDate&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:31:&quot;Thu, 24 Nov 2016 00:05:16 +0000&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}s:32:&quot;http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/&quot;;a:1:{s:7:&quot;creator&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;Jeff Chandler&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}}}i:13;a:6:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;
	
	
	
	
	
	
&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:30:&quot;HeroPress: Building Confidence&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:56:&quot;https://heropress.com/?post_type=heropress-essays&amp;p=1457&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:49:&quot;https://heropress.com/essays/building-confidence/&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:5817:&quot;&lt;img width=&quot;960&quot; height=&quot;480&quot; src=&quot;http://20094-presscdn.pagely.netdna-cdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/112316-1024x512.jpg&quot; class=&quot;attachment-large size-large wp-post-image&quot; alt=&quot;Pull Quote: The WordPress community give me confidence to talk to people &amp; in front of people.&quot; /&gt;&lt;p&gt;I can clearly divide my life in two parts before and after marriage. Before I got married, I was staying in Chapra, a small city in Bihar. I had graduated in Botany, we only had electricity for 5-6 hours a day and no easy access to internet or computers.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;After my marriage, I came to Pune, a bigger city compared to Chapra and things changed for me. I was exposed to exciting world of technology, thanks to my husband who was then working at a startup. I had lot of free time so I decided to learn as much as I could just to see what I can do and started by learning MS Office, then Photoshop a bit and in the process I also learned HTML, CSS. I tried but couldn’t get much hang of JavaScript.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Once I got confident that I can write decent HTML, I switched to learning CMS and first one I tried was Joomla, and for me it was very hard to understand, I had more question then I could find answers to. So on suggestion of my husband I switched to WordPress. I was able to quickly figure things out with WordPress and set up a blog for myself.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In 2010, I joined WPoets as QA. In those days I had some free time.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;To improve my skills I started looking into old reviews of the themes that were approved on WordPress themes repository.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;This helped me understand theme file structures and various criteria to check themes for, I used skills acquired to ensure themes built by WPoets were also following these guidelines.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Sometime in 2011, once I was confident that I have understood the process I joined the ‘Theme Review Team’ and started officially reviewing themes in the repository. This was a proud moment for me.  During my journey as theme reviewer I was helped and guided by Emil Uzelac, Chip Bennett &amp;amp; Edward Caissie.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In 2013, very first WordCamp was organised in Pune and I got a chance to talk about theme review process, this was my first ever public talk, and not being very good with English I choose to speak in Hindi. It was well received and many people wanted to know how they can get their themes approved. Again in 2015, I talked about what makes themes good in &lt;a href=&quot;https://2015.pune.wordcamp.org/&quot;&gt;WordCamp Pune&lt;/a&gt;. Thanks to WordCamps, I got to meet &lt;a href=&quot;https://heropress.com/essays/making-family/&quot;&gt;Topher&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=&quot;https://heropress.com/essays/breaking-the-silence/&quot;&gt;Mahangu&lt;/a&gt; &amp;amp; &lt;a href=&quot;https://heropress.com/essays/finding-hope-darkness/&quot;&gt;Raghvendra&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Now a days, as I get less time between work and kids, instead of doing theme reviews I answer questions on WordPress.org support forum.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;I&amp;#8217;m an introvert and came from a small city so I&amp;#8217;m always hesitant to talk to new people but the WordPress community give me confidence to talk with new people and in front of people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;This is a big achievement for me and my family feels proud of it.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In WP community every one ready to support and help to move forward because of this nature I love to this community. I want to emphasize the support that I have received from WordPress community in general and members of theme review team in specific who helped me gain the knowledge necessary to do my work better. I also want to thank all the organisers of Pune WordPress Knowledge Exchange meetup group, and specially &lt;a href=&quot;https://heropress.com/essays/ill-keep-looking-for-a-cms-unless-i-find-wordpress/&quot;&gt;Saurabh Shukla&lt;/a&gt; who helped in improving my presentation skills for WordCamp Pune.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;All these happened because of WordPress community and via HeroPress platform I would like to thank everyone who makes this community rock.&lt;/p&gt;

appeared first on &lt;a rel=&quot;nofollow&quot; href=&quot;https://heropress.com&quot;&gt;HeroPress&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:7:&quot;pubDate&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:31:&quot;Wed, 23 Nov 2016 12:00:44 +0000&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}s:32:&quot;http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/&quot;;a:1:{s:7:&quot;creator&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:11:&quot;Nisha Singh&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}}}i:14;a:6:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;
	
	
	
	
	
	
&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:87:&quot;WPTavern: Automattic Clarifies .blog Landrush Process After Bait and Switch Allegations&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:29:&quot;https://wptavern.com/?p=63947&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:97:&quot;https://wptavern.com/automattic-clarifies-blog-landrush-process-after-bait-and-switch-allegations&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:5998:&quot;&lt;p&gt;Earlier this year, Knock Knock Whois There LLC, an Automattic subsidiary in partnership with Primer Nivel, &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/automattic-to-oversee-the-sale-and-registration-of-top-level-blog-domains&quot;&gt;won an auction&lt;/a&gt; for around $19 million dollars to offer top-level .blog domains. On August 18th, an email was sent to users who signed up to &lt;a href=&quot;https://dotblog.wordpress.com/&quot;&gt;Dotblog.WordPress.com&lt;/a&gt; notifying them that they could apply and secure a .blog domain name before November 21st.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;a href=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/ApplyForBlogDomain.png?ssl=1&quot;&gt;&lt;img class=&quot;size-full wp-image-63953&quot; src=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/ApplyForBlogDomain.png?resize=480%2C781&amp;ssl=1&quot; alt=&quot;Applying For a Domain Name&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;Applying For a Domain Name
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;http://chrisschidle.com&quot;&gt;Chris Schidle&lt;/a&gt; took advantage of the opportunity and secured chris.blog for $30 per year with a $220 application fee. People who apply for a domain only receive it if no one else applies for it. If there are multiple applications, the domain goes through an auction process between November 14-17.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;As the auction dates drew nearer and Schidle didn&amp;#8217;t receive any information concerning the auction, he contacted support. Support confirmed that his application was not successful and he received a refund on November 15th. After asking support about the auction process, Schidle was informed that chris.blog ended up on a list of reserved domains that were not available for registration.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In a blog post entitled &amp;#8220;&lt;a href=&quot;http://chrisschidle.com/the-dot-blog-bait-and-switch/&quot;&gt;The .blog Bait and Switch&amp;#8221;&lt;/a&gt;, Schidle expressed disappointment in Automattic&amp;#8217;s lack of communication. &amp;#8220;Perhaps it&amp;#8217;s not fair to call this bait and switch,&amp;#8221; Schidle said.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Really it was bait and refund, and certainly the situation would be far worse had they chosen to not make the application fee refundable. But still, I thought I had a chance at securing the domain. That was the logical conclusion given the terms they outlined via a successful application or winning an auction.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Other applicants &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/cschidle/status/799287789430849536&quot;&gt;shared similar experiences&lt;/a&gt; on Twitter.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/cschidle&quot;&gt;@cschidle&lt;/a&gt; i feel your pain. they also took my $250 for my app for &lt;a href=&quot;https://t.co/8H0dBZfKny&quot;&gt;https://t.co/8H0dBZfKny&lt;/a&gt; &amp;#8211; surprisingly poor handling for a comm's company&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; Chris Yim (@cyim) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/cyim/status/799343938024968193&quot;&gt;November 17, 2016&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/cschidle&quot;&gt;@cschidle&lt;/a&gt; I've got stood up in the same manner for &lt;a href=&quot;https://t.co/wqDOQWyF2X&quot;&gt;https://t.co/wqDOQWyF2X&lt;/a&gt; Full-refund and no invitation to auction&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; Octavian Cioaca (@octasimo) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/octasimo/status/799295000240648192&quot;&gt;November 17, 2016&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/cschidle&quot;&gt;@cschidle&lt;/a&gt; Same thing happened to me with &lt;a href=&quot;https://t.co/1bRlWkdtmy&quot;&gt;https://t.co/1bRlWkdtmy&lt;/a&gt;. Not cool.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; Mark Barrera (@mark_barrera) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/mark_barrera/status/799366369733210114&quot;&gt;November 17, 2016&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In response to Schidel&amp;#8217;s post, Paolo Belcastro &lt;a href=&quot;https://my.blog/2016/11/17/about-reserved-domains/&quot;&gt;published an explanation&lt;/a&gt; of the process behind activating some domains in the Founder’s Program while reserving others. Belcastro says that as a registrar, they&amp;#8217;re able to activate up to 100 domain names. Some of the domains were given to &lt;a href=&quot;https://my.blog/founders/&quot;&gt;third-parties&lt;/a&gt; and 25 generic domains were given to WordPress.com to be shared for free with millions of users.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The registrar reserved all one, two, and three-character domains from being registered. They also allowed Automattic employees to reserve a single domain each, some of which were first names.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;On behalf of .blog, Belcastro apologized to those who filed applications in August and later discovered the domains were not available.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many registrars started taking pre-registrations for the Landrush period as early as last August. We do realize that some users were disappointed when they discovered that the domain names they had applied for were in fact attributed as part of the Founder’s program, or reserved, and wouldn’t be possible to register or auction at the end of Landrush.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;We would like to apologize to these users, but as the lists of Founder domains and Reserved ones weren’t final until just before Landrush, we couldn’t communicate them to registrars in advance (there is nothing registrars hate more than ever-changing lists of reserved domains).&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In addition, domains were removed as well as added to the lists, and we didn’t want to take the risk for registrars to refuse applications in September for domains that would be released in October.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;To mitigate the uncertainty surrounding domain availability, fees were set up in a way so that only successful registrations would be charged. This provided a way to give full refunds to those with failed applications.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Schidle appreciates the company&amp;#8217;s apology, &amp;#8220;It&amp;#8217;s unfortunate that their reserved domain list wasn&amp;#8217;t finalized prior to accepting applications, and that affected applicants like myself weren&amp;#8217;t notified sooner (auctions were scheduled to begin on November 14th),&amp;#8221; he said. &amp;#8220;But I think they realize their mistake in handling that communication and their apology is appreciated.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;div id=&quot;epoch-width-sniffer&quot;&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:7:&quot;pubDate&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:31:&quot;Sat, 19 Nov 2016 00:03:05 +0000&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}s:32:&quot;http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/&quot;;a:1:{s:7:&quot;creator&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;Jeff Chandler&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}}}i:15;a:6:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;
	
	
	
	
	
	
&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:86:&quot;WPTavern: WordPress Passes 27% Market Share, Banks on Customizer for Continued Success&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:29:&quot;https://wptavern.com/?p=63735&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:95:&quot;https://wptavern.com/wordpress-passes-27-market-share-banks-on-customizer-for-continued-success&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:18465:&quot;&lt;a href=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/paint-brushes.jpg?ssl=1&quot;&gt;&lt;img src=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/paint-brushes.jpg?resize=960%2C510&amp;ssl=1&quot; alt=&quot;photo credit: Luis Llerena&quot; class=&quot;size-full wp-image-63978&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;photo credit: &lt;a href=&quot;https://stocksnap.io/photo/265GAALCWG&quot;&gt;Luis Llerena&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;p&gt;WordPress now powers &lt;a href=&quot;https://w3techs.com/technologies/details/cm-wordpress/all/all&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;27.1% of all websites on the internet&lt;/a&gt;, up from 25% last year. While it may seem that WordPress is neatly adding 2% of the internet every year, its percentage increase fluctuates from year to year and the climb is getting more arduous with more weight to haul.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;a href=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/wordpress-market-share.png?ssl=1&quot;&gt;&lt;img src=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/wordpress-market-share.png?resize=1025%2C256&amp;ssl=1&quot; alt=&quot;credit: w3techs.com&quot; class=&quot;size-full wp-image-63933&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;credit: &lt;a href=&quot;https://w3techs.com/technologies/history_overview/content_management/all/y&quot;&gt;w3techs.com&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In January 2015, Mullenweg said the next goal for WordPress was &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/how-important-is-jetpack-on-wordpress-road-to-50-market-share&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;to achieve 50% market share&lt;/a&gt; (the majority of websites) and he identified Jetpack as a key factor in preventing WordPress&amp;#8217; decline, a controversial statement delivered at Pressnomics. At that time Automattic was secretly working on &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/early-reviews-show-applications-like-calypso-are-the-future-of-wordpress&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Calypso&lt;/a&gt;, WordPress.com&amp;#8217;s JavaScript-powered interface, but did not unveil the project until November 2015.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;It&amp;#8217;s difficult to say what effect Calypso has had on WordPress&amp;#8217; market share, as the w3tech&amp;#8217;s 27% stat covers mostly self-hosted sites. Following up with him a year later, Mullenweg estimates that less than 10% of those sites are hosted on WordPress.com.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;It does look like about a quarter of it is using Jetpack, though, and that has grown since Calypso was released,&amp;#8221; he said. &amp;#8220;Remember &amp;#8211; Calypso is for Jetpack sites as well as WP.com.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In a recent &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/wpweekly-episode-240-interview-with-matt-mullenweg-2016&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;interview on WPWeekly&lt;/a&gt;, Mullenweg said he is also optimistic that the WooCommerce acquisition and &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/automattic-to-oversee-the-sale-and-registration-of-top-level-blog-domains&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Automattic&amp;#8217;s sale and management of the .blog domain&lt;/a&gt; extension will contribute &amp;#8220;another 5-10% each to that market share.&amp;#8221; In fact, there is a team inside Automattic called Team 51 that works on strategies for getting the market share to 51%.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;For getting to 51% and beyond &amp;#8211; it&amp;#8217;s more than just blogs and more than just websites,&amp;#8221; Mullenweg said. &amp;#8220;We need to do stores well, we need to do wikis well, we need to do real estate sites well, we need to do restaurants well &amp;#8211; all these things that may be outside what you normally think of as a core WP experience.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In order to provide the best content-creation experience on the market, in any niche, WordPress has some major work to do. The software is in imminent danger of being eclipsed by newer competitors if its core features don&amp;#8217;t improve, especially when it comes to customizing a new site. Jetpack cannot single-handedly solve WordPress&amp;#8217; onboarding problem.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3&gt;WordPress&amp;#8217; Weakest Link Is Also Its Greatest Opportunity&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In the past Mullenweg has identified customization as the weakest link in WordPress but also one of its most important areas for improvement, saying, &amp;#8220;The Customizer is everything.&amp;#8221; During the &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/state-of-the-word-2015-javascript-and-api-driven-interfaces-are-the-future-of-wordpress&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;2015 State of the Word&lt;/a&gt; address he said, &amp;#8220;Customization is the single biggest opportunity for improving the WordPress experience.&amp;#8221; I asked him if he thinks the necessary improvements to make the software more competitive need to come from core itself or if commercial products could introduce game changers for the Customizer, the editor, and other problem areas of WordPress.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;I think to have an impact on WordPress&amp;#8217; growth improvements to customization have to happen in core or Calypso/Jetpack, otherwise there isn&amp;#8217;t enough reach,&amp;#8221; Mullenweg said. &amp;#8220;It doesn&amp;#8217;t matter how great a commercial product is &amp;#8211; being behind a paywall will mean it won&amp;#8217;t reach enough people to make a dent in WP&amp;#8217;s growth curve.&amp;#8221; He outlined how he sees the Customizer as a critical component of WordPress&amp;#8217; future:&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;I think the needed improvements will only come from a customizer and theme system which is flexible, intuitive, and instantaneous, which might also need to break backwards compatibility, and a post editor which leverages the same language, patterns, interface, and concepts. We need to use and adopt a React / Redux approach to the javascript, like Calypso, and rename or make irrelevant concepts like Menus which are just plain confusing to people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Customizer improvements have been a focus throughout 2016 and the feature&amp;#8217;s small team of contributors have made major strides towards improving the underlying architecture to build upon.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;It&amp;#8217;s &lt;em&gt;customization&lt;/em&gt; that is the key need here, not necessarily the existing &amp;#8216;Customizer&amp;#8217; interface that we have today,&amp;#8221; &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/components/customize/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Customize&lt;/a&gt; component maintainer Weston Ruter said. &amp;#8220;The key need WordPress has is to be able to live preview more functionality in WordPress, as Helen tweeted here:&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;What if instead of opposing &quot;shoving more stuff into the customizer&quot; we supported &quot;adding live previews to WordPress functionality&quot;?&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; Helen 侯-Sandí (@helenhousandi) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/helenhousandi/status/778255682462183424&quot;&gt;September 20, 2016&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/customizer-responsive-preview-and-selective-refresh-to-be-merged-into-wordpress-4-5&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Selective refresh&lt;/a&gt; was added earlier this year in 4.5, giving WordPress the ability to preview elements without full page reloads. This is one way the Customize API addresses that &amp;#8220;instantaneous&amp;#8221; aspect Mullenweg outlined above.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;The &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.xwp.co/2016/04/29/customize-posts-v0-5-released/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Customize Posts&lt;/a&gt; plugin is an effort to rebuild the post editor from the ground up with live preview for all changes to post data and postmeta at the foundation,&amp;#8221; Ruter said. &amp;#8220;It&amp;#8217;s a JavaScript-first approach to the post editing experience. No more waiting for a full page reload to save a draft. No more clicking a preview button to load the post in a new window. All of the changes are saved as drafts and previewed live. It&amp;#8217;s reusing the TinyMCE editor component.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;WordPress may be looking at &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/customize-posts-plugin-and-selective-refresh-are-paving-the-way-for-front-end-editing-powered-by-the-customizer&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;front-end editing powered by the Customizer&lt;/a&gt; in the not-too-distant future. Ruter and contributors are also working towards using the Customize API to power the next generation of widgets in core, which would use JS for UI and open the door for widgets to be managed via the REST API.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Changesets, coming in 4.7, is the most far-reaching architectural improvement to the customizer since its inception,&amp;#8221; Ruter said. &amp;#8220;Changesets allow for live preview functionality to be de-coupled from the current Customizer interface. This architecture allows for live preview to be developed in other contexts, namely on the frontend and in REST API-driven apps and headless sites.&amp;#8221; Ruter anticipates the REST API endpoints for managing changesets to come in 4.8.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href=&quot;https://github.com/xwp/wp-js-widgets&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;WP JS Widgets&lt;/a&gt; project shifts widgets away from their heavy reliance on PHP and creates a JavaScript foundation in the Customizer that allows for any library to be used to build widget controls.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;A control can actually be implemented using any JS technology stack,&amp;#8221; Ruter said. &amp;#8220;In the JS Widgets plugin I have a demonstration of widget controls in the customizer being built using the customizer Element, Backbone.js, and also React.&amp;#8221; The plugin currently implements &lt;a href=&quot;https://github.com/xwp/wp-js-widgets/tree/master/php/widgets&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;three core widgets&lt;/a&gt; and contributors are working on more.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;It&amp;#8217;s somewhat of a &amp;#8216;Widgets 3.0&amp;#8217; in core, focused on what they would look like in the Customizer first,&amp;#8221; Ruter said, &amp;#8220;where the widget instance data is stored in a setting, and the control listens to changes to that setting and updates its UI to reflect the changes in the underlying instance data.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Although this particular project is being built to be JavaScript-library agnostic, Ruter said he thinks there would be value in exploring the use of React/Redux in a “Customizer 2.0” interface:&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;It would be great to experiment with re-implementing the Customizer UI (pane, panels, sections, controls) in React. &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/hashtag/wcus?src=hash&quot;&gt;#wcus&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; Weston Ruter ⚡ (@westonruter) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/westonruter/status/672830761872064513&quot;&gt;December 4, 2015&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3&gt;Taking the Customizer Beyond First Impressions&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Ruter said he would have a difficult time proposing to build a client site that depended on the WordPress admin for site management and content authorship.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;A lot of the work we&amp;#8217;ve done at XWP for the past few years has been building on the Customizer to provide the editorial experiences that I think are lacking in the WP admin,&amp;#8221; Ruter said. &amp;#8220;We invest heavily in the customizer because we see it as the best foundation that WordPress has to offer to provide the experiences we want to deliver to our clients.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;If the Customizer is so critical to WordPress&amp;#8217; success, it&amp;#8217;s curious that the contributor team remains relatively small and few companies are investing in the feature specifically. &lt;a href=&quot;https://xwp.co/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;XWP&lt;/a&gt; invests heavily in Customizer development and a great deal of that is prototype work in the form of feature plugins such as Customize Snapshots, Customize Posts, and several &lt;a href=&quot;https://github.com/xwp?q=wp-customize&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;other plugin projects&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;I don&amp;#8217;t know for sure why more developers across the WordPress community aren&amp;#8217;t doing more with the customizer,&amp;#8221; said Ruter, who is CTO at XWP. &amp;#8220;It may be in part a perception problem, where it has seemingly been stuck with a negative reputation. Or it could be a technology problem, where the Customizer is vastly different from other areas of WordPress being a JavaScript single-page application.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;When the Customizer was first introduced it included support for &amp;#8216;option&amp;#8217; and &amp;#8216;theme_mod,&amp;#8217; followed by widgets and navigation menus a bit down the road. The WordPress community didn&amp;#8217;t have a full understanding of the scope and capabilities of the Customizer for addressing some of the project&amp;#8217;s chief concerns, including content authorship with live previews. Users simply saw a constrained UI they didn&amp;#8217;t like using to customize themes. Most users had no concept of the Customizer providing the architectural underpinnings of other aspects of WordPress.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Part of the problem is a lack of contributors,&amp;#8221; Ruter said. &amp;#8220;But even more than that, the biggest problem is one of having a shared vision. When the WP community throws hate on customizer improvements with each new release, it&amp;#8217;s somewhat demotivating.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;If the community realized the vision behind and appreciated a full admin experience that had live preview and staging of changes, then that would make a huge difference,&amp;#8221; Ruter said. He and contributors find it challenging to articulate the scope and they are considerably short-handed.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;It could be a problem with getting ramped up,&amp;#8221; he said. &amp;#8220;But that&amp;#8217;s been the mandate for the WP community as a whole &amp;#8211; to learn JavaScript deeply. I say, why not learn JavaScript deeply by building on the customizer?&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3&gt;GoDaddy Plans to Invest in Core Customizer Improvements&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;GoDaddy is a surprising company to emerge over the past couple of years as another key player on WordPress&amp;#8217; road to 50% market share. WordPress product and service companies, along with hosts, are keenly interested in seeing the software continue its market dominance.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;It&amp;#8217;s very important to GoDaddy that WP market share increases,&amp;#8221; Gabe Mays, head of WordPress products at GoDaddy, said. &amp;#8220;We&amp;#8217;re deeply invested in the success of the WordPress community, not only because many of our customers use it, but also because of what it represents.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Mays reports that approximately 1/3 of all GoDaddy sites and half of all new sites are running on WordPress. With millions of customers using the software, the company took the initiative to create a new &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/godaddy-launches-new-onboarding-experience-for-wordpress-customers&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;onboarding experience specifically for WordPress customers&lt;/a&gt;. I asked Mays if GoDaddy is concerned about competitors like Wix and Weebly cutting into the WordPress market share.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;I don&amp;#8217;t know if &amp;#8216;worry&amp;#8217; is the right word, because 1) we know the problem and 2) we&amp;#8217;re capable of addressing it,&amp;#8221; Mays said. &amp;#8220;As a community we either care enough to fix it or we don&amp;#8217;t. There are negative consequences associated with the latter that pose an existential threat to the community.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Mays has been involved with the WordPress community for the past 10 years and is working with GoDaddy to improve the new user experience.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Part of the issue we’re experiencing now is that WordPress benefitted from such strong product market fit and, subsequently, such high growth that there weren’t the typical feedback mechanisms forcing us to make things like the UX better,&amp;#8221; Mays said. &amp;#8220;On the other hand, our competitors fought hard and iterated for every basis point of market share they have. We’re like that natural athlete that got to the top with natural talent, but now need to master the basics, get our form right, etc. to stay on top as the game changes.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;GoDaddy&amp;#8217;s new onboarding experience is a stab at improving this UX and Mays said the company has significant WordPress core improvements in the works to provide a better first-use experience.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;I’m happy to say we’ll also be contributing these improvements back to core,&amp;#8221; he said. &amp;#8220;We&amp;#8217;re happy to have had Aaron Campbell join us as our first core contributor earlier this year and we&amp;#8217;re working on more ways to accelerate our core contribution efforts.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Mays said GoDaddy is planning to submit core contributions to the Customizer and related core components in the near future but would not disclose any specifics at this time.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Hosts benefit immensely from WordPress and as hosts we can collectively do a lot more to move WordPress forward,&amp;#8221; Mays said. &amp;#8220;We want to lead by example here and have big plans for this in 2017.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;WordPress product and service companies are also deeply invested in the software&amp;#8217;s success and eager to see its market share grow. Cory Miller, CEO of &lt;a href=&quot;https://ithemes.com/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;iThemes&lt;/a&gt;, one of WordPress&amp;#8217; earliest theme companies, said growth is one potential sign of WordPress&amp;#8217; health.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;“As WordPress has grown, so have we with it,&amp;#8221; Miller said. &amp;#8220;The opposite of growth is scary — it’s stagnation or decline. Many have predicted that in WordPress for a long time, but it continues to grow and push new limits, like the defiant teenager it is.”&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Miller said one of his concerns with competitors in the space is that they have started to cut into the lower  or lowest end of the market, &amp;#8220;the do-it-yourselfers that many of us count as a key part of our customer base.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;WordPress is fantastic, and so are all the innovative tools and services around it, from themes, plugins to hosting,&amp;#8221; Miller said. &amp;#8220;But I&amp;#8217;m increasingly seeing the reason why many, on perhaps the bottom end of the market, say, &amp;#8216;I don’t want to mess with updates, or worrying about security, or pulling 15 separate tools together to do what I need. I just want a simple website, I want everything in one bundle, mostly done for me, and so I’ll just go with SquareSpace or Weebly.'&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Although WordPress provides so much more in terms of community and extensibility when compared to Wix, Weebly, and Squarespace, getting started is the hardest part for new users. This is where tools like Jetpack and GoDaddy&amp;#8217;s onboarding experience are useful for giving users what they need to succeed with WordPress.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Even with a strong background in HTML and CSS, customizing a WordPress site can be challenging. Your site will never feel like your home on the web if you struggle to customize it to suit your preferences. The good news is that the WordPress project lead, contributors, hosts, and product companies are working together to improve customization in different, complementary ways in order to ensure the future of the project in an increasingly competitive market.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;div id=&quot;epoch-width-sniffer&quot;&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:7:&quot;pubDate&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:31:&quot;Fri, 18 Nov 2016 22:48:59 +0000&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}s:32:&quot;http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/&quot;;a:1:{s:7:&quot;creator&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;Sarah Gooding&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}}}i:16;a:6:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;
	
	
	
	
	
	
&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:97:&quot;WPTavern: 2nd Global WordPress Translation Day Brings 780 Translators Together Across 133 Locales&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:29:&quot;https://wptavern.com/?p=63915&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:108:&quot;https://wptavern.com/2nd-global-wordpress-translation-day-brings-780-translators-together-across-133-locales&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:6919:&quot;&lt;p&gt;The second &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptranslationday.org/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Global WordPress Translation Day&lt;/a&gt; was held November 12 and the &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/polyglots/2016/11/16/notes-from-the-polyglots-chats-on-november-16th/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;stats&lt;/a&gt; released this week show the event was an even greater success than the first one. In April, &lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/global-wordpress-translation-day-draws-448-participants-from-105-countries&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;WP Translation Day connected 448 participants&lt;/a&gt; through both online and in-person translation events. The second event brought 780 translators together, a 74% increase in participation. Attendance at the local events increased from 39 in April to 67 in November.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;Looking at &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/hashtag/WPTranslationDay?src=hash&quot;&gt;#WPTranslationDay&lt;/a&gt; stats I can’t be more grateful to &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/humanmadeltd&quot;&gt;@humanmadeltd&lt;/a&gt; for supporting my time for the &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/hashtag/WordPress?src=hash&quot;&gt;#WordPress&lt;/a&gt; Polyglots. So lucky❤️ &lt;a href=&quot;https://t.co/NwSjYOyUBX&quot;&gt;pic.twitter.com/NwSjYOyUBX&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; Petya Raykovska (@petyeah) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/petyeah/status/798890778692988929&quot;&gt;November 16, 2016&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;We really wanted to build on top of what was already there, reach more people, and bring more important topics front and center,&amp;#8221; said Petya Raykovska, one of the members of the Polyglots Leadership Team. Participants had the opportunity to discuss the upcoming internationalization features in WordPress with core developers, including a session by Pascal Birchler and a panel led by John Blackbourn. Translators also discussed gender neutral languages in the WordPress UI, prompted by a discussion around gender neutral German.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Raykovska said one of the goals of the second event was to &amp;#8220;bring more people on screen so everyone can feel like they&amp;#8217;re a part of a truly global event.&amp;#8221; Local participation for the live streaming meetups increased from April.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;The activities in India have kept their strong growth rate &amp;#8211; we had eight events last time, this time they were 14, with Mumbai even having two events,&amp;#8221; Raykovska said. &amp;#8220;For the first time we had events in Russia and in South Africa.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Raykovska said she&amp;#8217;s hoping the Polyglots Leadership Team will soon begin developing events in African regions, following patterns of success in Asia and Europe.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote class=&quot;twitter-tweet&quot;&gt;&lt;p lang=&quot;en&quot; dir=&quot;ltr&quot;&gt;Thank you &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/ResellerClub&quot;&gt;@ResellerClub&lt;/a&gt; for hosting us today for &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/hashtag/WPTranslationday?src=hash&quot;&gt;#WPTranslationday&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href=&quot;https://t.co/vfr9l0D50V&quot;&gt;pic.twitter.com/vfr9l0D50V&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;mdash; WordCamp Mumbai (@WCMumbai) &lt;a href=&quot;https://twitter.com/WCMumbai/status/797397743467659264&quot;&gt;November 12, 2016&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h3&gt;Global WordPress Translation Day Expands Into South Africa&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;a href=&quot;https://i2.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/cape-town-wptranslation-day.jpg?ssl=1&quot;&gt;&lt;img src=&quot;https://i2.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/cape-town-wptranslation-day.jpg?resize=1025%2C548&amp;ssl=1&quot; alt=&quot;Cape Town WP Translation Day - credit: Jon Bossenger&quot; class=&quot;size-full wp-image-63927&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;Cape Town WP Translation Day &amp;#8211; credit: &lt;a href=&quot;https://wpcapetown.co.za/2016/11/14/wordpress-global-translation-day-recap/&quot;&gt;Jon Bossenger&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;p&gt;South Africa has 11 languages and Raykovska said the event gave a big boost to the translation community there, with Xhosa being translated for WordPress for the first time. Xhosa is spoken by 7.6 million people, which is approximately 18% of the South African population.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Africa has a huge potential and a lot of wonderful, enthusiastic people,&amp;#8221; she said. &amp;#8220;There will be more WordCamps there in 2017 and hopefully more activity on the translation side.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Jon Bossenger and Hugh Lashbrooke, who co-organized the Cape Town event, had attendees translating WordPress into Xhosa, Sotho, and Setswana.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;By the end of the day we had two translation files for these languages that we’ll be looking to submit requests to be added as locales for WordPress,&amp;#8221; Bossenger said in his recap &lt;a href=&quot;https://wpcapetown.co.za/2016/11/14/wordpress-global-translation-day-recap/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;post&lt;/a&gt;. &amp;#8220;We’re almost halfway towards adding all 11 official languages, just in one day.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Trisha Cornelius, co-organizer the WP Johannesburg Meetup, organized the in-person translation event in Johannesburg where the team made major progress and assisted the Cape Town team in getting their languages started.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;We managed to get Xhosa approved in time for us to translate some strings for our translation day event,&amp;#8221; Cornelius said. &amp;#8220;We translated into Afrikaans (which is at over 95% so we are pushing to get to 100%) and South African English as well. People who were at the Cape Town event have volunteered to become translators for Tswana and an attendee at our event has volunteered to become GTE for Zulu.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Cornelius said the excitement of live streaming and connecting with Cape Town and others around the world could have easily made for an event that spanned longer than three hours.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;The biggest success was in showing people how easy translation is,&amp;#8221; Cornelius said. She hopes the translations will make WordPress easier for more people in South Africa and help more people get onto the web.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;South Africa has an interesting case in that we have a clear racial split between being multi-lingual and not,&amp;#8221; Cornelius said. &amp;#8220;The majority of our population speaks English/Afrikaans as a second language and I would love to see WordPress available in all 11 of our official languages so that WP users can choose to use WP in their mother tongue.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Cornelius said language accessibility in South Africa is less of a challenge than actual access to computers with the Internet, but they are making progress in Johannesburg to make the internet more widely available.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Especially with the prevalence of smart phones, I think having WordPress in more indigenous languages will mean that we are able to hear more of each others voices which we desperately need,&amp;#8221; Cornelius said.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Overall, the WordPress Translation Day participants translated a total of 60,426 strings, up from 40,350 in April. This includes popular plugins and themes in addition to WordPress core. A video replay of the event can be found on &lt;a href=&quot;https://www.crowdcast.io/e/gwtd2&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Crowdcast.io&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;

&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:77:&quot;WPTavern: WPWeekly Episode 254 – WP eCommerce, WordCamp US 2017, and Upvato&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:58:&quot;https://wptavern.com?p=63903&amp;preview=true&amp;preview_id=63903&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:82:&quot;https://wptavern.com/wpweekly-episode-254-wp-ecommerce-wordcamp-us-2017-and-upvato&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:2450:&quot;&lt;p&gt;In this short episode of WordPress Weekly, &lt;a href=&quot;http://marcuscouch.com/&quot;&gt;Marcus Couch&lt;/a&gt; and I discuss a handful of news stories making headlines. We discuss a recent security update to the WP eCommerce plugin, Upvato disappearing only to reappear in the near future, and Nashville, TN hosting WordCamp US in 2017 and 2018. Last but not least is Marcus&amp;#8217; plugin picks of the week.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h2&gt;Stories Discussed:&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/wp-ecommerce-3-11-4-patches-sql-injection-vulnerability&quot;&gt;WP eCommerce 3.11.4 Patches SQL Injection Vulnerability&lt;/a&gt;&lt;br /&gt;
&lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/upvato-backup-service-confirms-files-are-lost-plans-to-relaunch-on-new-provider&quot;&gt;Upvato Backup Service Confirms Files Are Lost, Plans to Relaunch on New Provider&lt;/a&gt;&lt;br /&gt;
&lt;a href=&quot;https://wptavern.com/nashville-to-host-wordcamp-us-2017-2018&quot;&gt;Nashville to Host WordCamp US 2017-2018&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;h2&gt;Plugins Picked By Marcus:&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/plugins/show-featured-image-size-in-admin-topbar&quot;&gt;Show Featured Image Size in Admin TopBar&lt;/a&gt; displays the image size for the featured image in the admin top bar. This makes it convenient to know how large features images need to be without looking it up.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/plugins/wp-mqtt/&quot;&gt;WP-MQTT&lt;/a&gt; connects WordPress to the Internet of Things. This plugin can automatically send MQTT messages to compatible devices when something happens on your site. MQTT is a machine-to-machine (M2M) / Internet of Things connectivity protocol. It was designed as an extremely lightweight publish/subscribe messaging transport.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/plugins/hello-trumpy/&quot;&gt;Hello Trumpy&lt;/a&gt; is a plugin aimed at making WordPress great again!&lt;/p&gt;

&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:80:&quot;WPTavern: Visible Edit Shortcuts in WordPress 4.7 Makes Customizing Sites Easier&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:29:&quot;https://wptavern.com/?p=63881&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:91:&quot;https://wptavern.com/visible-edit-shortcuts-in-wordpress-4-7-makes-customizing-sites-easier&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:3913:&quot;&lt;p&gt;Earlier this year, Automattic added visual icons to the WordPress.com customizer after user testing showed users tried clicking on the parts of the page they wanted to edit, rather than searching through the menus in the customizer. In an effort to see if the &lt;a href=&quot;https://core.trac.wordpress.org/ticket/27403&quot;&gt;same could be done&lt;/a&gt; for the self-hosted version of WordPress, &lt;a href=&quot;https://core.trac.wordpress.org/ticket/27403#comment:16&quot;&gt;Payton Swick open sourced&lt;/a&gt; Automattic&amp;#8217;s work into a plugin and &lt;a href=&quot;https://github.com/Automattic/customize-direct-manipulation&quot;&gt;placed it on GitHub&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The plugin added persistent icons to show users which parts of a site can be customized when the customizer preview pane is open. After months of collaboration between Automatticians and the Customize component maintainers, the icons were merged into WordPress 4.7 and are officially called visible edit shortcuts. The icons visually inform users which elements can be edited in a theme.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The icons appear when the customizer is open and directs users to settings that control an element. For example, clicking the icon next to the site tagline in the image below opens the Site Identity section of the customizer and makes the Tagline field active. Visible shortcuts are an extension of the Shift-click to edit keyboard shortcut that was added in WordPress 3.9.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;a href=&quot;https://i2.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/CustomizerShortcutButtons.png?ssl=1&quot;&gt;&lt;img class=&quot;size-full wp-image-63883&quot; src=&quot;https://i2.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/CustomizerShortcutButtons.png?resize=1025%2C544&amp;ssl=1&quot; alt=&quot;Visible Edit Shortcut Buttons&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;Visible Edit Shortcut Buttons
&lt;p&gt;The icon approach was largely inspired by WordPress.com which has a similar feature in its customizer. Nick Halsey, Customize component maintainer, &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/2016/11/10/visible-edit-shortcuts-in-the-customizer-preview/&quot;&gt;describes in detail&lt;/a&gt; the history of the feature and what theme authors need to do to support it.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Unlike many of the theme specific updates in the past where developers can add support by using add_theme_support, supporting visible edit shortcuts is more comprehensive. Theme authors will need to add support for &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/2016/02/16/selective-refresh-in-the-customizer/&quot;&gt;selective refresh&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/2016/03/22/implementing-selective-refresh-support-for-widgets/&quot;&gt;selective refresh for widgets&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/2015/07/29/fast-previewing-changes-to-menus-in-the-customizer/&quot;&gt;selective refresh for menus&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Edit shortcuts will be enabled by default for all themes, but are contingent on themes supporting selective refresh,&amp;#8221; Halsey said. Additionally, a small amount of CSS may be required to properly position the icons.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Adding visual elements that connect parts of a theme to the customizer should take some of the guesswork out of editing themes. Instead of spending time browsing through various customizer panels to edit a part of a site, users can click a button and the right customizer panel will open automatically with the settings you need. This can be especially useful for themes that have a lot of customizer sections.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;I tested visible edit shortcuts using the Twenty Seventeen theme in WordPress 4.7 beta 4 and didn&amp;#8217;t encounter any issues. The team is strongly encouraging users to test with as many themes as possible. If you use a theme where the shortcut icons are not displayed, please contact the theme author, request that they add support, and refer them to &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/2016/11/10/visible-edit-shortcuts-in-the-customizer-preview/&quot;&gt;the make core blog post which explains how to do so&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;div id=&quot;epoch-width-sniffer&quot;&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:7:&quot;pubDate&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:31:&quot;Thu, 17 Nov 2016 08:54:19 +0000&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}s:32:&quot;http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/&quot;;a:1:{s:7:&quot;creator&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;Jeff Chandler&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}}}i:20;a:6:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;
	
	
	
	
	
	
&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:49:&quot;WPTavern: Nashville to Host WordCamp US 2017-2018&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:29:&quot;https://wptavern.com/?p=63871&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:60:&quot;https://wptavern.com/nashville-to-host-wordcamp-us-2017-2018&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:5593:&quot;&lt;a href=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/nashville.jpg?ssl=1&quot;&gt;&lt;img src=&quot;https://i0.wp.com/wptavern.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/nashville.jpg?resize=1015%2C524&amp;ssl=1&quot; alt=&quot;photo credit: Viv Lynch Westward - (license)&quot; class=&quot;size-full wp-image-63884&quot; /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;photo credit: Viv Lynch &lt;a href=&quot;http://www.flickr.com/photos/68894626@N00/30070498810&quot;&gt;Westward&lt;/a&gt; &amp;#8211; &lt;a href=&quot;https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/2.0/&quot;&gt;(license)&lt;/a&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&quot;https://2016.us.wordcamp.org/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;WordCamp US&lt;/a&gt; attendees are counting down the days until the event kicks off in Philadelphia in two weeks but preparations for 2017 and 2018 are already underway. Yesterday Matt Mullenweg &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/news/2016/11/wordcamp-us-2017-2018-in-nashville/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;announced&lt;/a&gt; that Nashville has won the bid to host WordCamp US for the next two years. According to Randy Hicks, one of the organizers, the new &lt;a href=&quot;http://www.nashvillemusiccitycenter.com/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;Music City Center&lt;/a&gt; venue, which was finished in 2013, has been reserved from Thursday, November 30, 2017, to Sunday, December 3 but the camp will take place Friday &amp;#8211; Sunday. The venue has confirmed the ability to host 3,000 &amp;#8211; 5,000 attendees.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;We have a brand new venue that is pretty amazing but Nashville is very centrally located to handful of other cities that all have their own WordCamps,&amp;#8221; Hicks said. &amp;#8220;I think there are about seven camps within 4-5 hours. The WP community around Nashville is rather strong.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Over the past few years the local WordPress community has grown and WordCamp Nashville sold 325 tickets at its 2016 event.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;I&amp;#8217;ve been coming to the meetups since the very first one and have been an organizer since about the same time as well as John Housholder,&amp;#8221; Hicks said. &amp;#8220;We&amp;#8217;ve seen the community explode every year after WordCamp, but 2014 and 2015 have been huge growth years for Nashville as a whole and the meetup has reflected those numbers.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The application process included nailing down a venue, creating a budget, and gathering specific details about wifi capabilities, room capabilities, hotel availability, and date availability. Organizers from both the Nashville team and the Denver team (another finalist) agreed that the application time frame was somewhat constrained.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;I thought the time frame between start and submission was pretty short, but I think that depends on who is submitting and how informed they are on their local venue,&amp;#8221; Hicks said. &amp;#8220;Ours was really hard to get information from.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The Denver team had a similar struggle with locking down a venue without certainty of being able to fully book the reservation.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;There was what I would consider to be a semi-fanatical obsession with the first weekend in December, which was flatly unavailable at the convention center here,&amp;#8221; said Drew Jaynes, one of the organizers who applied on behalf of Denver.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;To give you some perspective, at the time that we applied, March 2016, there were two available weekends left for the Colorado Convention Center &amp;#8211; two in all of 2017. The end of August and the middle of December. To organize an event of this size on what would be considered relatively short notice for a city as popular as Denver was essentially a fool&amp;#8217;s errand.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The Nashville organizing team was able to secure its venue for early December dates, but a wider range of acceptable dates might be one way for WordCamp Central to improve the process for next year. This would give more cities the opportunity to submit competitive applications, as venues that can accommodate the expected size of WCUS are in high demand in popular cities.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;We had a great set of communities apply so it was tough to pick just one,&amp;#8221; WordCamp Central representative Cami Kaos said. &amp;#8220;In the end we went with Nashville for WordCamp US 2017-2018 because it seems like a great location for attendees, they had a beautiful venue that could accommodate an event of this size as we grow, and the dynamics between both teams were a natural fit.&amp;#8221;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;If you&amp;#8217;re interested in learning more about Nashville, the organizing team has created a &lt;a href=&quot;http://wpnashville.com/wcus/&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;page&lt;/a&gt; that includes some favorite local attractions and rankings:&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;ul&gt;
&lt;li&gt;Ranked within the top 10 on Cvent ’s list of Top 50 Meeting Destinations in the United States. (August 2015)&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;li&gt;Named #3 Best Convention City in the 10 Best USA Today Readers’ Choice Awards&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;li&gt;Ranked #1 Friendliest City in America by Travel and Leisure (April 2015)&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;li&gt;Listed in Collaborate Magazine’s list of the Top 12 Foodie Cities for Meetings&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;/ul&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Nashville is centrally located for a relatively short drive or flight from anywhere in the US and should have ample options for accommodation. The city won the bid to host the WordCamp out of six applicants and Matt Mullenweg&amp;#8217;s announcement post indicates that there were multiple local communities capable of hosting the event in the future.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&amp;#8220;Based on the other great applications we got I’m also excited about the pipeline of communities that could host it in future years as WordCamp US travels across the United States and gives us an opportunity to learn and love a new city, as we have with Philadelphia,&amp;#8221; Mullenweg said.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;div id=&quot;epoch-width-sniffer&quot;&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:7:&quot;pubDate&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:31:&quot;Wed, 16 Nov 2016 21:50:56 +0000&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}s:32:&quot;http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/&quot;;a:1:{s:7:&quot;creator&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;Sarah Gooding&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}}}}i:21;a:6:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:13:&quot;
	
	
	
	
	
	
&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:55:&quot;HeroPress: Actually, WordPress didn’t change my life.&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:56:&quot;https://heropress.com/?post_type=heropress-essays&amp;p=1490&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:66:&quot;https://heropress.com/essays/actually-wordpress-didnt-change-life/&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:9579:&quot;&lt;img width=&quot;960&quot; height=&quot;480&quot; src=&quot;http://20094-presscdn.pagely.netdna-cdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/11/111616-1024x512.jpg&quot; class=&quot;attachment-large size-large wp-post-image&quot; alt=&quot;Pull Quote: If we serve the people around what we're doing, we ourselves will grow, develop &amp; change alongside the people we serve.&quot; /&gt;&lt;p&gt;My story starts in high school as a girl with a technical bent, in a small country town just as computers were becoming mainstream but well before they were in everyone’s home. (I was in high school when Apple came to school and showed off this new fangled thing they called a mouse…). We were taught BASIC and Pascal and I actually really enjoyed tinkering with programming, but no one thought to say, “Dee, you look like you’re good at this, you should pursue it…” I mean, I was a girl (and girls didn’t ‘DO’ computers), and no one in the circles I moved in really had any idea where this technology revolution would take us.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The truth is I wasn’t particularly ambitious, I didn’t have any kind of clue what I wanted to be ‘when I grew up’ so I allowed myself to be gently steered by my parents into leaving school before the end of my final year when I was encouraged to apply for a job in a bank.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;I got the job and started in the MICR processing department, encoding cheques with magnetic text, and I finished as a teller 3 years later (and boy was I terrible at balancing the books at the end of the day &amp;#8211; I was much better at chatting to the customers!).&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Financial independence was the only name of that game; for me there was very little else to recommend the job, so I saved all the money I could and took off from both the bank, and my homeland (New Zealand) in very short order as a young, naive 20 year old primed to spend the next 3 years exploring the world. And by the world, I mean the US and Europe…&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;That first mouse had come out in 1983, WordPress was founded in 2003 and in those 20 years the world changed. While it was doing that I was at various times being a nanny, working in child care centres, working in customer support, temping, and generally &amp;#8216;working to live&amp;#8217; in whatever way felt right at the time. However, in 1999 I packed up my bags once again, moved from New Zealand to Australia and took a place at a performing arts school where I honed my singing and performance skills and volunteered my time to our music director who was starting to experiment with sending out HTML newsletters and updates via email.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;And so my personal revolution began.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;On what I think was the day after I graduated from that course I walked into a full time role as that music director’s assistant and began my journey back to code. It was part of my job to edit those HTML newsletters and send them out every week. I went from there to buying books about coding for the web, experimenting on my home built PC making web pages. I’m sure like a lot of us, I remember the thrill of creating that first HTML file and seeing a ‘Hello World’ or similar heading rendered in the browser… from there I was completely hooked.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;By 2004 I was working full time as a webmaster, by 2005 I was running a small business creating sites on the side of my ‘real job’ and by 2009 that small business became my full time job as I left employment to pursue my Masters Degree in Digital Communication. It was in that year I met WordPress when I moved my old Movable Type blog onto it and within a very short time I was using  WP as the CMS of choice for all my client work…&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Then in 2011 I stumbled across WordCamps and by extension the WordPress community. THAT was the thing that changed my world.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;I flew on a whim from Sydney to Melbourne to attend this crazy inexpensive conference I had found after a google search for ‘WordPress Conferences’. In doing so I met welcoming people, made friends, connected, and came back to Sydney excited and hopeful about continuing this connection with the wider WP community.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Building a community around WP got off to a slow start in Sydney, but from an inauspicious early WPSyd meetup in the function room of a pub (one we actually held in the dark due to a blackout) my connection and involvement took off. Before long I was helping organise that Sydney meetup and by the time I moved away from that great city it had branched into 2 meetups, and soon after, into 3. Furthermore at the end of that first year, at my second ever WordCamp, I put my hand up to help organise a WordCamp Sydney in 2012 and then as I moved interstate, WordCamp Melbourne in 2013.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Here’s the point.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;WordPress, software, technology, the Internet will come and go, morph, and change, evolve. Maybe WordPress will last forever, maybe it will morph into something else, maybe one day it will look completely different than it did when I first started (actually, that’s true now…) the thing that doesn’t change is the humanity around it.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;WordPress and any other software package exists to serve people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The thing that I have learned, not only through WordPress but in life, is that if we too serve the people around what we’re doing, we ourselves will grow, develop and change alongside the people we serve, and the tools we use to serve them.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In organising meetups, WordCamps, and community events around WordPress,  I have found that at the end of the day it isn’t actually WordPress that matters… it’s those connections, it’s the friendships. It’s the network of work, referrals, support, help, encouragement that has kept me wired into this community and committed to helping other people find that connection and growth for themselves. Even as my career in WP has moved through coding, into team support, into project management, there too it has been the people and my connections to them in the community that have helped fuel that transition.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;I believe that the place I’ve found and the opportunities I have had owe as much to my own desire and ambition as they do to the help, support and belief of the community around me; sometimes even more than I’ve felt in myself. In all of this I feel as though I’m living proof that by helping, connecting, and resourcing other people, I myself have been helped, resourced and connected into places I’d have never dreamed of all those years ago as a teenager playing with that first mouse… experimenting with that BASIC code.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Arnold Schwarzenegger said “Help others and give something back. I guarantee you will discover that while public service improves the lives and the world around you, its greatest reward is the enrichment and new meaning it will bring your own life.” and I completely agree.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Ricky Martin said “A hero is someone who is willing to help others in his or her best capacity.” Here are two very different people giving voice to exactly what I’ve found in working and serving in this great community of diverse people. This is the kind of heroism I see around WordPress, at meetups and WordCamps; it’s the kind of hero whom I want to raise up, and at the end of the day, this is the kind of hero I hope that I am.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Best of all, it’s the kind of hero that absolutely ANY of us can be.&lt;/p&gt;

&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:5:&quot;child&quot;;a:2:{s:0:&quot;&quot;;a:5:{s:5:&quot;title&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:30:&quot;Dev Blog: WordPress 4.7 Beta 4&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;guid&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:34:&quot;https://wordpress.org/news/?p=4576&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:4:&quot;link&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:56:&quot;https://wordpress.org/news/2016/11/wordpress-4-7-beta-4/&quot;;s:7:&quot;attribs&quot;;a:0:{}s:8:&quot;xml_base&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;s:17:&quot;xml_base_explicit&quot;;b:0;s:8:&quot;xml_lang&quot;;s:0:&quot;&quot;;}}s:11:&quot;description&quot;;a:1:{i:0;a:5:{s:4:&quot;data&quot;;s:1990:&quot;&lt;p&gt;WordPress 4.7 Beta 4 is now available!&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;This software is still in development,&lt;/strong&gt; so we don’t recommend you run it on a production site. Consider setting up a test site just to play with the new version. To test WordPress 4.7, try the &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/plugins/wordpress-beta-tester/&quot;&gt;WordPress Beta Tester&lt;/a&gt; plugin (you’ll want “bleeding edge nightlies”). Or you can &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/wordpress-4.7-beta4.zip&quot;&gt;download the beta here&lt;/a&gt; (zip).&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;For more information on what’s new in 4.7, check out the &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/news/2016/10/wordpress-4-7-beta-1/&quot;&gt;Beta 1,&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/news/2016/11/wordpress-4-7-beta-2/&quot;&gt;Beta 2&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/news/2016/11/wordpress-4-7-beta-3/&quot;&gt;Beta 3&lt;/a&gt; blog posts, along with &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/tag/4-7+dev-notes/&quot;&gt;in-depth developer guides on make/core&lt;/a&gt;. We’ve made &lt;a href=&quot;https://core.trac.wordpress.org/log/trunk/src?action=stop_on_copy&amp;mode=stop_on_copy&amp;rev=39262&amp;stop_rev=39201&amp;limit=200&amp;verbose=on&amp;sfp_email=&amp;sfph_mail=&quot;&gt;about 60 changes&lt;/a&gt; in the last few days for beta 4, including tweaks to Twenty Seventeen, custom CSS, and the REST API content endpoints.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Do you speak a language other than English? &lt;a href=&quot;https://translate.wordpress.org/projects/wp/dev&quot;&gt;Help us translate WordPress into more than 100 languages!&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;If you think you’ve found a bug&lt;/strong&gt;, you can post to the &lt;a href=&quot;https://wordpress.org/support/forum/alphabeta&quot;&gt;Alpha/Beta area&lt;/a&gt; in the support forums. We’d love to hear from you! If you’re comfortable writing a reproducible bug report, &lt;a href=&quot;https://make.wordpress.org/core/reports/&quot;&gt;file one on WordPress Trac&lt;/a&gt;, where you can also find &lt;a href=&quot;https://core.trac.wordpress.org/tickets/major&quot;&gt;a list of known bugs&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;We are almost there&lt;br /&gt;
Please test your plugins and themes&lt;br /&gt;
